<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Inuit</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For the 2010 film, see <a href="/wiki/Inuk_(film)" title="Inuk (film)">Inuk (film)</a>. For other uses of Inuit, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Inuit_(disambiguation)" title="Inuit (disambiguation)">Inuit (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innu_people" title="Innu people">Innu people</a>, a First Nations people in eastern Quebec and Labrador.</div>

<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Indigenous_Peoples_of_Canada" title="Template:Indigenous Peoples of Canada"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Indigenous_Peoples_of_Canada" title="Template talk:Indigenous Peoples of Canada"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Indigenous_Peoples_of_Canada&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <b>Inuit</b> (pronounced <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/uː/ or /ʊ/: 'u' in 'influence'">u</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="/nj/: 'n' in 'new'">nj</span><span title="/uː/ or /ʊ/: 'u' in 'influence'">u</span><span title="/./: syllable break">.</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>/</a></span></span>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuktitut_language" title="Inuktitut language">Inuktitut</a>: <span lang="iu"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%90%83%E1%93%84%E1%90%83%E1%91%A6" title="wikt:ᐃᓄᐃᑦ">ᐃᓄᐃᑦ</a></span>, "the people"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naming_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-naming-7">[7]</a></sup>) are a group of culturally similar <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">indigenous peoples</a> inhabiting the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> regions of <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Inuit is a plural noun; the singular is <b>Inuk</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naming_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-naming-7">[7]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Inuit_languages" title="Inuit languages">Inuit languages</a> are part of the <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" title="Eskimo–Aleut languages">Eskimo-Aleut family</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Sign_Language" title="Inuit Sign Language">Inuit Sign Language</a> is a critically endangered <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AmsterdamLing_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-AmsterdamLing-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the United States and Canada, the term "<a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a>" was commonly used to describe the Inuit and Alaska's <a href="/wiki/Yupik" title="Yupik">Yupik</a> and <a href="/wiki/I%C3%B1upiat" title="Iñupiat">Iñupiat</a> peoples. However, "Inuit" is not accepted as a term for the Yupik, and "<a href="/wiki/Eskimo" title="Eskimo">Eskimo</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaplan_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-kaplan-11">[11]</a></sup> is the only term that includes <a href="/wiki/Yupik" title="Yupik">Yupik</a>, <a href="/wiki/I%C3%B1upiat" title="Iñupiat">Iñupiat</a> and Inuit. However, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">aboriginal peoples in Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_Inuit" title="Greenlandic Inuit">Greenlandic Inuit</a> view "Eskimo" as <a href="/wiki/Pejorative" title="Pejorative">pejorative</a>, and "Inuit" is more commonly used in self-reference for these groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartleby_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-bartleby-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natlang_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-natlang-13">[13]</a></sup> In Canada, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Section_Twenty-five_of_the_Canadian_Charter_of_Rights_and_Freedoms" title="Section Twenty-five of the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms">sections 25</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Section_Thirty-five_of_the_Constitution_Act,_1982" title="Section Thirty-five of the Constitution Act, 1982">35</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Constitution_Act,_1982" title="Constitution Act, 1982">Constitution Act of 1982</a> classified the Inuit as a distinctive group of Aboriginal Canadians who are not included under either the <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" title="Métis people (Canada)">Métis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defe_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-defe-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Inuit live throughout most of <a href="/wiki/Northern_Canada" title="Northern Canada">Northern Canada</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">territory</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavik" title="Nunavik">Nunavik</a> in the northern third of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunatsiavut" title="Nunatsiavut">Nunatsiavut</a> and <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a> in <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a>, and in various parts of the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>, particularly around the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>. These areas are known in <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a> as the "Inuit Nunangat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/I%C3%B1upiat" title="Iñupiat">Iñupiat</a> live primarily on the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_North_Slope" title="Alaska North Slope">Alaska North Slope</a> and on <a href="/wiki/Little_Diomede_Island" title="Little Diomede Island">Little Diomede Island</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_Inuit" title="Greenlandic Inuit">Greenlandic Inuit</a> are descendants of indigenous migrations from <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. They are citizens of <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a>, although not of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Precontact_history">Precontact history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Precontact history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For their earlier precontact history, see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada#Paleo-Indians_period" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal peoples in Canada § Paleo-Indians period</a>.</div>
<div class="floatleft"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arctic_cultures_900-1500.png" title="Arctic cultures from 900 CE to 1500 CE"><img alt="Maps showing the different cultures (Dorset, Thule, Norse, Innu, and Beothuk) in Greenland, Labrador, Newfoundland and the Canadian arctic islands in the years 900, 1100, 1300 and 1500" data-file-height="989" data-file-width="259" height="764" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Arctic_cultures_900-1500.png/200px-Arctic_cultures_900-1500.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/Arctic_cultures_900-1500.png 1.5x" width="200"/></a></div>
<p>Inuit are the descendants of what <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a> call the <a href="/wiki/Thule_people" title="Thule people">Thule culture</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TriggerWashburn1996_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-TriggerWashburn1996-17">[17]</a></sup> who emerged from western <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a> around 1000 CE. They had split from the related <a href="/wiki/Aleut" title="Aleut">Aleut</a> group about 4,000 years ago and from northeastern Siberian migrants, possibly related to the <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_language" title="Chukchi language">Chukchi language</a> group, still earlier. They spread eastwards across the Arctic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeanMatthews1998_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-DeanMatthews1998-18">[18]</a></sup> They displaced the related <a href="/wiki/Dorset_culture" title="Dorset culture">Dorset culture</a>, the last major <a href="/wiki/Paleo-Eskimo" title="Paleo-Eskimo">Paleo-Eskimo</a> culture (in <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a>, called the <i>Tuniit</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColeGentilcore1987_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColeGentilcore1987-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inuit legends speak of the Tuniit as "giants", people who were taller and stronger than the Inuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Less frequently, the legends refer to the Dorset as "dwarfs".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> Researchers believe that the Dorset culture lacked the dogs, larger weapons and other technologies of the Inuit society, which gave the latter an advantage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diamond2006_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Diamond2006-22">[22]</a></sup> By 1300, Inuit migrants had reached west Greenland, where they settled, moving into east Greenland over the following century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffecker2005_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffecker2005-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p>Faced with population pressures from the Thule and other surrounding groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">Algonquian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siouan_languages" title="Siouan languages">Siouan</a> to the south, the Tuniit gradually receded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> They were thought to have become completely extinct as a people by about 1400 or 1500.</p>
<p>But, in the mid-1950s, researcher Henry B. Collins determined that, based on the ruins found at <a href="/wiki/Native_Point" title="Native Point">Native Point</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sadlermiut" title="Sadlermiut">Sadlermiut</a> were likely the last remnants of the Dorset culture, or Tuniit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The Sadlermiut population survived up until winter 1902–03, when exposure to new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">infectious diseases</a> brought by contact with Europeans led to their extinction as a people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 21st century, <a href="/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup" title="Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup">mitochondrial DNA</a> research has supported the theory of continuity between the Tuniit and the Sadlermiut peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> It also provided evidence that a population displacement did not occur within the <a href="/wiki/Aleutian_Islands" title="Aleutian Islands">Aleutian Islands</a> between the Dorset and Thule transition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> In contrast to other Tuniit populations, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleut_people" title="Aleut people">Aleut</a> and Sadlermiut benefited from both geographical isolation and their ability to adopt certain Thule technologies.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, Inuit circulated almost exclusively north of the "<a href="/wiki/Tree_line#Types_of_tree_lines" title="Tree line">Arctic tree line</a>", the effective southern border of Inuit society. The most southern "officially recognized" Inuit community in the world is <a href="/wiki/Rigolet" title="Rigolet">Rigolet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> in Nunatsiavut.</p>
<p>South of Nunatsiavut, the descendants of the southern <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> Inuit in NunatuKavut continued their traditional <a href="/wiki/Transhumance" title="Transhumance">transhumant</a> semi-nomadic way of life until the mid-1900s. The Nunatukavummuit people usually moved among islands and bays on a seasonal basis. They did not establish stationary communities. In other areas south of the tree line, <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">Native American</a> and First Nations cultures were well established. The culture and technology of Inuit society that served so well in the <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">Arctic</a> were not suited to subarctic regions, so they did not displace their southern neighbors.</p>
<p>Inuit had trade relations with more southern cultures; boundary disputes were common and gave rise to aggressive actions. Warfare was not uncommon among those Inuit groups with sufficient population density. Inuit such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nunatamiut" title="Nunatamiut">Nunatamiut</a> (<i><a href="/wiki/Uummarmiut" title="Uummarmiut">Uummarmiut</a></i>), who inhabited the <a href="/wiki/Mackenzie_River" title="Mackenzie River">Mackenzie River</a> delta area, often engaged in warfare. The more sparsely settled Inuit in the Central Arctic, however, did so less often.</p>
<p>Their first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europeans" title="Europeans">European</a> contact was with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Viking" title="Viking">Vikings</a> who settled in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_Greenland" title="Norse Greenland">Greenland</a> and explored the eastern <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canadian</a> coast. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_sagas" title="Norse sagas">Norse sagas</a> recorded meeting <i><a href="/wiki/Skr%C3%A6ling" title="Skræling">skrælingar</a>,</i> probably an undifferentiated label for all the indigenous peoples whom the Norse encountered, whether Tuniit, Inuit, or <a href="/wiki/Beothuk" title="Beothuk">Beothuk</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weidensaul2012_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weidensaul2012-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>After about 1350, the climate grew colder during the period known as the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>. During this period, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaskan</a> natives were able to continue their <a href="/wiki/Whaling" title="Whaling">whaling</a> activities. But, in the high Arctic, the Inuit were forced to abandon their hunting and whaling sites as <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whale" title="Bowhead whale">bowhead whales</a> disappeared from <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PerrinWursig2009_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-PerrinWursig2009-32">[32]</a></sup> These Inuit had to subsist on a much poorer diet, and lost access to the essential raw materials for their tools and architecture which they had previously derived from whaling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PerrinWursig2009_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-PerrinWursig2009-32">[32]</a></sup> The changing climate forced the Inuit to work their way south, forcing them into marginal niches along the edges of the tree line. These were areas which Native Americans had not occupied or where they were weak enough for the Inuit to live near them. Researchers have difficulty defining when Inuit stopped this territorial expansion. There is evidence that they were still moving into new territory in southern Labrador when they first began to interact with <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">Europeans</a> in the 17th century.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Postcontact_history">Postcontact history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Postcontact history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_(1753)_(14577039230).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2022" data-file-width="3310" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg/220px-Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg/330px-Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg/440px-Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_%281753%29_%2814577039230%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Histoire_de_l%27Amerique_Septentrionale_-_divis%C3%A9e_en_quatre_tomes_(1753)_(14577039230).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A European ship coming into contact with the Inuit in the ice of Hudson Bay in 1697.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_contact_with_Europeans">Early contact with Europeans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early contact with Europeans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The lives of Paleo-Eskimos of the far north were largely unaffected by the arrival of visiting Norsemen except for mutual trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Labrador Inuit have had the longest continuous contact with Europeans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> After the disappearance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norse_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="Norse colonization of the Americas">Norse colonies in Greenland</a>, the Inuit had no contact with Europeans for at least a century. By the mid-16th century, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basque_people" title="Basque people">Basque</a> whalers and fishermen were already working the Labrador coast and had established whaling stations on land, such as the one that has been excavated at <a href="/wiki/Red_Bay,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Red Bay, Newfoundland and Labrador">Red Bay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The Inuit appear not to have interfered with their operations, but they raided the stations in winter for tools and items made of worked iron, which they adapted to their own needs. <a href="/wiki/Martin_Frobisher" title="Martin Frobisher">Martin Frobisher</a>'s 1576 search for the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Passage" title="Northwest Passage">Northwest Passage</a> was the first well-documented post-<a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Columbian</a> contact between Europeans and Inuit. Frobisher's expedition landed in <a href="/wiki/Frobisher_Bay" title="Frobisher Bay">Frobisher Bay</a>, Baffin Island, not far from the settlement now called The City of <a href="/wiki/Iqaluit" title="Iqaluit">Iqaluit</a> which was long known as <i>Frobisher Bay</i>. Frobisher encountered Inuit on <a href="/wiki/Resolution_Island_(Nunavut)" title="Resolution Island (Nunavut)">Resolution Island</a> where five sailors left the ship, under orders from Frobisher, and became part of Inuit mythology. The homesick sailors, tired of their adventure, attempted to leave in a small vessel and vanished. Frobisher brought an unwilling Inuk to <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, possibly the first Inuk ever to visit Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The Inuit oral tradition, in contrast, recounts the natives helping Frobisher's crewmen, whom they believed had been abandoned.</p>
<p>The semi-nomadic eco-centred Inuit were fishers and hunters harvesting lakes, seas, ice platforms and <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>. While there are some allegations that Inuit were hostile to early <a href="/wiki/France" title="France">French</a> and English explorers, fishers and whalers, more recent research suggests that the early relations with whaling stations along the Labrador coast and later <a href="/wiki/James_Bay" title="James Bay">James Bay</a> were based on a mutual interest in trade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> In the final years of the 18th century, the Moravian Church began missionary activities in Labrador, supported by the British who were tired of the raids on their whaling stations. The Moravian missionaries could easily provide the Inuit with the iron and basic materials they had been stealing from whaling outposts, materials whose real cost to Europeans was almost nothing, but whose value to the Inuit was enormous and from then on contacts in Labrador were far more peaceful.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg"><img alt="The Hudson's Bay Company Ships Prince of Wales and Eddystone with Inuit boats off the Upper Savage Islands, Hudson Strait, Canada" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="760" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg/220px-HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg/330px-HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg/440px-HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HBC-Upper_Savage_Islands-Hudson_Strait.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hudson's Bay Company Ships bartering with Inuit off the Upper Savage Islands, <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Strait" title="Hudson Strait">Hudson Strait</a>, 1819</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The European arrival tremendously damaged the Inuit way of life, causing mass death through new diseases introduced by whalers and explorers, and enormous social disruptions caused by the distorting effect of Europeans' material wealth. Nonetheless, Inuit society in the higher latitudes had largely remained in isolation during the 19th century. The <a href="/wiki/Hudson%27s_Bay_Company" title="Hudson's Bay Company">Hudson's Bay Company</a> opened <a href="/wiki/Trading_post" title="Trading post">trading posts</a> such as Great Whale River (1820), today the site of the twin villages of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Whapmagoostui,_Quebec" title="Whapmagoostui, Quebec">Whapmagoostui</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuujjuarapik,_Quebec" title="Kuujjuarapik, Quebec">Kuujjuarapik</a>, where whale products of the commercial whale hunt were processed and furs traded. The British Naval Expedition of 1821–3 led by Admiral <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Edward_Parry" title="William Edward Parry">William Edward Parry</a>, which twice over-wintered in <a href="/wiki/Foxe_Basin" title="Foxe Basin">Foxe Basin</a>, provided the first informed, sympathetic and well-documented account of the economic, social and religious life of the Inuit. Parry stayed in what is now <a href="/wiki/Igloolik" title="Igloolik">Igloolik</a> over the second winter. Parry's writings, with pen and ink illustrations of Inuit everyday life, and those of <a href="/wiki/George_Francis_Lyon" title="George Francis Lyon">George Francis Lyon</a>, both published in 1824 were widely read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> Captain <a href="/wiki/George_Comer" title="George Comer">George Comer</a>'s Inuit wife Shoofly, known for her sewing skills and elegant attire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> was influential in convincing him to acquire more sewing accessories and beads for trade with Inuit.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_20th_century">Early 20th century</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Early 20th century">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>During the early 20th century a few traders and missionaries circulated among the more accessible bands, and after 1904 they were accompanied by a handful of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> (RCMP). Unlike most Aboriginal peoples in Canada, however, the lands occupied by the Inuit were of little interest to European settlers — to the southerners, the homeland of the Inuit was a hostile <a href="/wiki/Hinterland" title="Hinterland">hinterland</a>. Southerners enjoyed lucrative careers as bureaucrats and service providers to the north, but very few ever chose to visit there. Canada, with its more hospitable lands largely settled, began to take a greater interest in its more peripheral territories, especially the fur and mineral-rich hinterlands. By the late 1920s, there were no longer any Inuit who had not been contacted by traders, missionaries or government agents. In 1939, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Canada" title="Supreme Court of Canada">Supreme Court of Canada</a> found, in a decision known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Re_Eskimos" title="Re Eskimos">Re Eskimos</a></i>, that the Inuit should be considered Indians and were thus under the jurisdiction of the federal government.</p>
<p>Native customs were worn down by the actions of the RCMP, who enforced <a href="/wiki/Criminal_law_of_Canada" title="Criminal law of Canada">Canadian criminal law</a> on Inuit, such as <a href="/wiki/Kikkik" title="Kikkik">Kikkik</a>, who often could not understand what they had done wrong, and by missionaries who preached a <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral code</a> very different from the one they were used to. Many of the Inuit were systematically converted to Christianity in the 19th and 20th centuries, through rituals like the <a href="/wiki/Siqqitiq" title="Siqqitiq">Siqqitiq</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_World_War_to_the_1960s">Second World War to the 1960s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Second World War to the 1960s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>World War II and the Cold War made Arctic Canada strategically important for the first time and, thanks to the development of modern aircraft, accessible year-round. The construction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_airbase" title="Military airbase">air bases</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Distant_Early_Warning_Line" title="Distant Early Warning Line">Distant Early Warning Line</a> in the 1940s and 1950s brought more intensive contacts with European society, particularly in the form of public education, which traditionalists complained instilled foreign values disdainful of the traditional structure of Inuit society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1950s the <a href="/wiki/High_Arctic_relocation" title="High Arctic relocation">High Arctic relocation</a> was undertaken by the <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Canada" title="Government of Canada">Government of Canada</a> for several reasons. These were to include protecting Canada's <a href="/wiki/Territorial_claims_in_the_Arctic" title="Territorial claims in the Arctic">sovereignty in the Arctic</a>, alleviating hunger (as the area currently occupied had been over-hunted), and attempting to solve the "Eskimo problem", meaning the assimilation and end of the Inuit culture. One of the more notable relocations was undertaken in 1953, when 17 families were moved from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inukjuak,_Quebec" title="Inukjuak, Quebec">Port Harrison</a> (now Inukjuak, Quebec) to <a href="/wiki/Resolute,_Nunavut" title="Resolute, Nunavut">Resolute</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grise_Fiord,_Nunavut" title="Grise Fiord, Nunavut">Grise Fiord</a>. They were dropped off in early September when winter had already arrived. The land they were sent to was very different from that in the Inukjuak area; it was barren, with only a couple of months when the temperature rose above freezing and several months of <a href="/wiki/Polar_night" title="Polar night">polar night</a>. The families were told by the RCMP they would be able to return within two years if conditions were not right. However, two years later more families were relocated to the High Arctic and it was to be thirty years before they were able to visit Inukjuak.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>By 1953, <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Canada" title="Prime Minister of Canada">Canada's prime minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Louis_St._Laurent" title="Louis St. Laurent">Louis St. Laurent</a> publicly admitted, "Apparently we have administered the vast territories of the north in an almost continuing absence of mind."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PK32_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-PK32-45">[45]</a></sup> The government began to establish about forty permanent administrative centres to provide education, health and economic development services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PK32_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-PK32-45">[45]</a></sup> Inuit from hundreds of smaller camps scattered across the north, began to congregate in these hamlets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regular visits from doctors, and access to modern medical care raised the <a href="/wiki/Birth_rate" title="Birth rate">birth rate</a> and decreased the <a href="/wiki/Mortality_rate" title="Mortality rate">death rate</a>, causing an enormous <a href="/wiki/Demographic_transition" title="Demographic transition">natural increase</a>. In the 1950s, the Canadian government began to actively settle Inuit into permanent villages and cities, occasionally against their will (such as in Nuntak and Hebron). These forced resettlements were acknowledged by the Canadian government in 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> By the mid-1960s, encouraged first by missionaries, then by the prospect of paid jobs and government services, and finally forced by hunger and required by police, most Canadian Inuit lived year-round in permanent settlements. The nomadic migrations that were the central feature of Arctic life had become a much smaller part of life in the North. The Inuit, a once self-sufficient people in an extremely harsh environment were, in the span of perhaps two generations, transformed into a small, impoverished minority, lacking skills or resources to sell to the larger economy, but increasingly dependent on it for survival.</p>
<p>Although anthropologists like <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Jenness" title="Diamond Jenness">Diamond Jenness</a> (1964) were quick to predict that Inuit culture was facing extinction, Inuit political activism was already emerging.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_renewal">Cultural renewal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Cultural renewal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the 1960s, the Canadian government funded the establishment of <a href="/wiki/Secularity" title="Secularity">secular</a>, government-operated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">high schools</a> in the Northwest Territories (including what is now Nunavut) and Inuit areas in Quebec and Labrador along with the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Indian_residential_school_system" title="Canadian Indian residential school system">residential school system</a>. The Inuit population was not large enough to support a full high school in every community, so this meant only a few schools were built, and students from across the territories were boarded there. These schools, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aklavik,_Northwest_Territories" title="Aklavik, Northwest Territories">Aklavik</a>, Iqaluit, <a href="/wiki/Yellowknife" title="Yellowknife">Yellowknife</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuvik,_Northwest_Territories" title="Inuvik, Northwest Territories">Inuvik</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kuujjuaq,_Quebec" title="Kuujjuaq, Quebec">Kuujjuaq</a>, brought together young Inuit from across the Arctic in one place for the first time, and exposed them to the rhetoric of <a href="/wiki/Civil_and_political_rights" title="Civil and political rights">civil</a> and <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human rights</a> that prevailed in Canada in the 1960s. This was a real wake-up call for the Inuit, and it stimulated the emergence of a new generation of young Inuit activists in the late 1960s who came forward and pushed for respect for the Inuit and their territories.</p>
<p>The Inuit began to emerge as a political force in the late 1960s and early 1970s, shortly after the first graduates returned home. They formed new politically active associations in the early 1970s, starting with the <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Tapiriit_Kanatami" title="Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami">Inuit Tapirisat of Canada</a> (Inuit Brotherhood and today known as Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami), an outgrowth of the Indian and Eskimo Association of the '60s, in 1971, and more region specific organizations shortly afterwards, including the Committee for the Original People's Entitlement (representing the Inuvialuit),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> the Northern Quebec Inuit Association (<a href="/wiki/Makivik_Corporation" title="Makivik Corporation">Makivik Corporation</a>) and the Labrador Inuit Association (LIA) representing Northern Labrador Inuit. Since the mid-1980s the Southern Labrador Inuit of <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a> began organizing politically after being geographically cut out of the LIA, however, for political expediency the organization was erroneously called the Labrador Métis Nation. These various activist movements began to change the direction of Inuit society in 1975 with the <a href="/wiki/James_Bay_and_Northern_Quebec_Agreement" title="James Bay and Northern Quebec Agreement">James Bay and Northern Quebec Agreement</a>. This comprehensive land claims settlement for Quebec Inuit, along with a large cash settlement and substantial administrative autonomy in the new region of Nunavik, set the precedent for the settlements to follow. The northern Labrador Inuit submitted their land claim in 1977, although they had to wait until 2005 to have a signed land settlement establishing <a href="/wiki/Nunatsiavut" title="Nunatsiavut">Nunatsiavut</a>. Southern Labrador Inuit of <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a> are currently in the process of establishing landclaims and title rights that would allow them to negotiate with the Newfoundland Government.</p>
<p>Canada's 1982 Constitution Act recognized the Inuit as Aboriginal peoples in Canada, but not <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-defe_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-defe-14">[14]</a></sup> In the same year, the <a href="/wiki/Tunngavik_Federation_of_Nunavut" title="Tunngavik Federation of Nunavut">Tunngavik Federation of Nunavut</a> (TFN) was incorporated, in order to take over negotiations for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aboriginal_land_claim" title="Aboriginal land claim">land claims</a> on behalf of the Inuit living in the eastern Northwest Territories, that would later become Nunavut, from the Inuit Tapiriit Kanatami, which became a joint association of the Inuit of Quebec, Labrador, and the Northwest Territories.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inuit_cabinet_members_at_the_federal_level">Inuit cabinet members at the federal level</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Inuit cabinet members at the federal level">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On October 30, 2008, <a href="/wiki/Leona_Aglukkaq" title="Leona Aglukkaq">Leona Aglukkaq</a> was appointed as <a href="/wiki/Minister_of_Health_(Canada)" title="Minister of Health (Canada)">Minister of Health</a>, "[becoming] the first Inuk to hold a senior cabinet position, although she is not the first Inuk to be in cabinet altogether."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jack_Anawak" title="Jack Anawak">Jack Anawak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nancy_Karetak-Lindell" title="Nancy Karetak-Lindell">Nancy Karetak-Lindell</a> were both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parliamentary_Secretary" title="Parliamentary Secretary">parliamentary secretaries</a> respectively from 1993 to 1996 and in 2003.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature">Nomenclature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Nomenclature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Eskimo#Nomenclature" title="Eskimo">Eskimo § Nomenclature</a></div>
<p>In the United States, the term "Eskimo" is still commonly used, because it includes Inuit, Aleut, Iñupiat, and Yupik peoples whilst distinguishing them from American Indians. The Yupik do not speak an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_language" title="Inuit language">Inuit language</a> nor consider themselves to be Inuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaplan_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-kaplan-11">[11]</a></sup> However, the term is probably a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Innu-aimun" title="Innu-aimun">Montagnais</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aueo_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-aueo-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-goddard_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-goddard-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-campbell_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-campbell-52">[52]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exonym" title="Exonym">exonym</a> as well as being widely used in<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aueo_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-aueo-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mailhot_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-mailhot-53">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-creeml_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-creeml-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-igoddard_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-igoddard-55">[55]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Folk_etymology" title="Folk etymology">folk etymology</a> as meaning "eater of raw meat" in the <a href="/wiki/Cree_language" title="Cree language">Cree language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natlang_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-natlang-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlebyeskimo_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-bartlebyeskimo-56">[56]</a></sup> It is now considered pejorative or even a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Racial_slur" title="Racial slur">racial slur</a> amongst the Canadian and English-speaking Greenlandic Inuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-natlang_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-natlang-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bartlebyeskimo_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-bartlebyeskimo-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Canada and Greenland, "Inuit" is preferred. <i>Inuit</i> is the <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Eastern Canadian Inuit</a> (Inuktitut) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalaallisut_language" title="Kalaallisut language">West Greenlandic</a> (Kalaallisut) word for "the people."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-naming_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-naming-7">[7]</a></sup> Since Inuktitut and Kalaallisut are the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prestige_dialect" title="Prestige dialect">prestige dialects</a> in Canada and Greenland, respectively, their version has become dominant, although every Inuit dialect uses cognates from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Eskimo" title="Proto-Eskimo">Proto-Eskimo</a> <i>*ińuɣ</i> – for example, "people" is <i>inughuit</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuktun_language" title="Inuktun language">North Greenlandic</a> and <i>iivit</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunumiisut_language" title="Tunumiisut language">East Greenlandic</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_history">Cultural history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cultural history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inuit_culture" title="Inuit culture">Inuit culture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg"><img alt="Inuktitut dialect map with labels in Inuktitut inuujingajut or local Roman alphabet" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="798" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg/300px-Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg/450px-Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg/600px-Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuktitut_dialect_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Distribution of Inuit dialects</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inuit_languages" title="Inuit languages">Inuit languages</a></div>
<p>Inuit speak <a href="/wiki/Inuinnaqtun" title="Inuinnaqtun">Inuinnaqtun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inuktitut" title="Inuktitut">Inuktitut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inuvialuktun" title="Inuvialuktun">Inuvialuktun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_language" title="Greenlandic language">Greenlandic languages</a>, which belong to the Inuit-Inupiaq branch of the <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" title="Eskimo–Aleut languages">Eskimo–Aleut language family.</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ethno_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-ethno-6">[6]</a></sup> The Greenlandic languages are divided into: <a href="/wiki/Kalaallisut" title="Kalaallisut">Kalaallisut</a> (Western), <a href="/wiki/Inuktun" title="Inuktun">Inuktun</a> (Northern), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunumiit_language" title="Tunumiit language">Tunumiit</a> (Eastern).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inuktitut is spoken in Canada and along with Inuinnaqtun is one of the official languages of Nunavut and are known collectively as the Inuit Language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang-58">[58]</a></sup> In the Northwest Territories, Inuvialuktun, Inuinnaqtun and Inuktitut are all official langues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lang-2_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-lang-2-59">[59]</a></sup> Kalaallisut is the official language of Greenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stern2004_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stern2004-60">[60]</a></sup> As Inuktitut was the language of the Eastern Canadian Inuit and Kalaallisut is the language of the Western Greenlandic Inuit, they are related more closely than most other dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorais2010_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorais2010-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inuit in Alaska and Canada also typically speak <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In Greenland, Inuit also speak <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and learn <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> in school. Canadian Inuit may also speak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quebecker_language" title="Quebecker language">Québécois French</a>.</p>
<p>Finally, Deaf Inuit speak <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Sign_Language" title="Inuit Sign Language">Inuit Sign Language</a>, often called Inuiuuk, which is a <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">language isolate</a> and almost extinct as only around 50 people still speak it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Diet">Diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_diet" title="Inuit diet">Inuit diet</a></div>
<p>The Inuit have traditionally been fishers and hunters. They still hunt <a href="/wiki/Whale" title="Whale">whales</a> (esp. <a href="/wiki/Bowhead_whale" title="Bowhead whale">bowhead whale</a>), <a href="/wiki/Walrus" title="Walrus">walrus</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribou_(North_America)" title="Caribou (North America)">caribou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polar_bear" title="Polar bear">polar bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/Muskox" title="Muskox">muskoxen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birds" title="Birds">birds</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fish" title="Fish">fish</a> and at times other less commonly eaten animals such as the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a>. The typical Inuit diet is high in <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">protein</a> and very high in <a href="/wiki/Animal_fat" title="Animal fat">fat</a> – in their traditional diets, Inuit consumed an average of 75% of their daily energy intake from fat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> While it is not possible to cultivate plants for food in the Arctic, the Inuit have traditionally gathered those that are naturally available. <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">Grasses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tuber" title="Tuber">tubers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Root" title="Root">roots</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plant_stem" title="Plant stem">stems</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berry" title="Berry">berries</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Seaweed" title="Seaweed">seaweed</a> (<i>kuanniq</i> or edible seaweed) were collected and preserved depending on the season and the location.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> There is a vast array of different <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_American_Hunting_Technologies" title="North American Hunting Technologies">hunting technologies</a> that the Inuit used to gather their food.</p>
<p>In the 1920s anthropologist <a href="/wiki/Vilhjalmur_Stefansson" title="Vilhjalmur Stefansson">Vilhjalmur Stefansson</a> lived with and studied a group of Inuit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA_Lieb_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA_Lieb-69">[69]</a></sup> The study focused on the fact that the Inuit's <a href="/wiki/Low-carbohydrate_diet" title="Low-carbohydrate diet">low-carbohydrate diet</a> had no adverse effects on their health, nor indeed, Stefansson's own health. Stefansson (1946) also observed that the Inuit were able to get the necessary vitamins they needed from their traditional winter diet, which did not contain any plant matter. In particular, he found that adequate <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a> could be obtained from items in their traditional diet of <a href="/wiki/Raw_animal_food_diets" title="Raw animal food diets">raw meat</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Ringed_seal" title="Ringed seal">ringed seal</a> <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> and whale skin (<a href="/wiki/Muktuk" title="Muktuk">muktuk</a>). While there was considerable skepticism when he reported these findings, they have been borne out in recent studies and analyses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> However, the Inuit have lifespans 12 to 15 years shorter than the average Canadian's, which is thought to be a result of limited access to medical services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuterslife_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-reuterslife-72">[72]</a></sup> The life expectancy gap is not closing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-reuterslife_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-reuterslife-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Fish_oil" title="Fish oil">fish oil supplement</a> studies have failed to support claims of preventing heart attacks or strokes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150917-cz_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150917-cz-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20150330_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20150330-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JAMA-201403_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-JAMA-201403-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Transport,_navigation,_and_dogs"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Transport.2C_navigation.2C_and_dogs">Transport, navigation, and dogs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Transport, navigation, and dogs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg"><img alt="Photograph of an Inuit man seated in a kayak, holding a paddle" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2791" data-file-width="3651" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg/220px-Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg/330px-Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg/440px-Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_man_by_Curtis_-_Noatak_AK.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inupiat in a kayak, Noatak, Alaska, c. 1929 (photo by <a href="/wiki/Edward_S._Curtis" title="Edward S. Curtis">Edward S. Curtis</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG/220px-Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG/330px-Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG/440px-Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Qinngorput-Nuuk.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Urbanization in <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The natives hunted <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">sea animals</a> from single-passenger, covered seal-skin boats called <i><a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">qajaq</a></i> (Inuktitut syllabics: <i>ᖃᔭᖅ</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livingdict_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-livingdict-78">[78]</a></sup> which were extraordinarily buoyant, and could easily be righted by a seated person, even if completely overturned. Because of this property, the design was copied by <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Europe" title="Demographics of Europe">Europeans</a> and Americans who still produce them under the Inuit name <a href="/wiki/Kayak" title="Kayak">kayak</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuitbasket.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1563" data-file-width="1693" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Inuitbasket.jpg/220px-Inuitbasket.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Inuitbasket.jpg/330px-Inuitbasket.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Inuitbasket.jpg/440px-Inuitbasket.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuitbasket.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inupiat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baleen_basket" title="Baleen basket">baleen basket</a>, with an ivory handle, made by Kinguktuk (1871–1941) of <a href="/wiki/Barrow,_Alaska" title="Barrow, Alaska">Barrow, Alaska</a>. Displayed at the <a href="/wiki/San_Diego_Museum_of_Man" title="San Diego Museum of Man">Museum of Man</a>, <a href="/wiki/San_Diego" title="San Diego">San Diego</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Inuit also made <i><a href="/wiki/Umiak" title="Umiak">umiaq</a></i> ("woman's boat"), larger open boats made of wood frames covered with animal skins, for transporting people, goods, and dogs. They were 6–12 m (20–39 ft) long and had a flat bottom so that the boats could come close to shore. In the winter, Inuit would also hunt <a href="/wiki/Marine_mammal" title="Marine mammal">sea mammals</a> by patiently watching an <i>aglu</i> (breathing hole) in the ice and waiting for the air-breathing seals to use them. This technique is also used by the polar bear, who hunts by seeking holes in the ice and waiting nearby.</p>
<p>In winter, both on land and on sea ice, the Inuit used <a href="/wiki/Dog_sled" title="Dog sled">dog sleds</a> (<i>qamutik</i>) for transportation. The <i><a href="/wiki/Husky" title="Husky">husky</a></i> dog breed comes from Inuit breeding of dogs and wolves for transportation. A <a href="/wiki/Sled_dog" title="Sled dog">team of dogs</a> in either a tandem/side-by-side or fan formation would pull a sled made of wood, animal bones, or the <a href="/wiki/Baleen" title="Baleen">baleen</a> from a whale's mouth and even frozen fish,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> over the snow and ice. The Inuit used stars to navigate at sea and landmarks to navigate on land; they possessed a comprehensive native system of <a href="/wiki/Toponymy" title="Toponymy">toponymy</a>. Where natural landmarks were insufficient, the Inuit would erect an <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inukshuk" title="Inukshuk">inukshuk</a></i>.</p>
<p>Dogs played an integral role in the annual routine of the Inuit. During the summer they became pack animals, sometimes dragging up to 20 kg (44 lb) of baggage and in the winter they pulled the sled. Yearlong they assisted with hunting by sniffing out seals' holes and pestering polar bears. They also protected the Inuit villages by barking at bears and strangers. The Inuit generally favored, and tried to breed, the most striking and handsome of dogs, especially ones with bright eyes and a healthy coat. Common husky dog breeds used by the Inuit were the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Eskimo_Dog" title="Canadian Eskimo Dog">Canadian Eskimo Dog</a>, the official animal of Nunavut,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> (<i>Qimmiq</i>; Inuktitut for dog), the <a href="/wiki/Greenland_Dog" title="Greenland Dog">Greenland Dog</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Husky" title="Siberian Husky">Siberian Husky</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Alaskan_Malamute" title="Alaskan Malamute">Alaskan Malamute</a>. The Inuit would perform rituals over the newborn pup to give it favorable qualities; the legs were pulled to make them grow strong and the nose was poked with a pin to enhance the sense of smell.</p>
<h3><span id="Industry,_art,_and_clothing"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Industry.2C_art.2C_and_clothing">Industry, art, and clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Industry, art, and clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_(UBC).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2958" data-file-width="1915" height="340" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg/220px-Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg/330px-Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg/440px-Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_%28UBC%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Early_20th_Century_Inuit_parka_(UBC).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inuit woman's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anorak" title="Anorak">parka</a>, Canada.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3416" data-file-width="4248" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg/220px-Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg/330px-Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg/440px-Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuit-Kleidung_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Traditional clothing; left: seal, right: caribou.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Inuit_art" title="Inuit art">Inuit art</a></div>
<p>Inuit industry relied almost exclusively on animal hides, <a href="/wiki/Driftwood" title="Driftwood">driftwood</a>, and bones, although some tools were also made out of worked stones, particularly the readily worked <a href="/wiki/Soapstone" title="Soapstone">soapstone</a>. <a href="/wiki/Walrus_ivory" title="Walrus ivory">Walrus ivory</a> was a particularly essential material, used to make knives. Art played a big part in Inuit society and continues to do so today. Small sculptures of animals and human figures, usually depicting everyday activities such as <a href="/wiki/Hunting" title="Hunting">hunting</a> and whaling, were carved from <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> and bone. In modern times <a href="/wiki/Printmaking" title="Printmaking">prints</a> and figurative works carved in relatively soft stone such as <a href="/wiki/Soapstone" title="Soapstone">soapstone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serpentinite" title="Serpentinite">serpentinite</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Argillite" title="Argillite">argillite</a> have also become popular.</p>
<p>Inuit made clothes and footwear from animal skins, sewn together using needles made from animal bones and threads made from other animal products, such as <a href="/wiki/Tendon" title="Tendon">sinew</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anorak" title="Anorak">anorak</a> (parka) is made in a similar fashion by Arctic peoples from Europe through <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and the Americas, including the Inuit. The hood of an <i><a href="/wiki/Amauti" title="Amauti">amauti</a></i>, (women's parka, plural <i>amautiit</i>) was traditionally made extra large with a separate compartment below the hood to allow the mother to carry a baby against her back and protect it from the harsh wind. Styles vary from region to region, from the shape of the hood to the length of the tails. Boots (<i><a href="/wiki/Mukluk" title="Mukluk">mukluk</a></i> or <i>kamik</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>), could be made of caribou or seal skin, and designed for men and women.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuit-Igloo.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4108" data-file-width="5073" height="178" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Inuit-Igloo.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Inuit-Igloo.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Inuit-Igloo.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Inuit-Igloo.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Inuit-Igloo.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Inuit-Igloo.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuit-Igloo.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inuit building an igloo</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the winter, certain Inuit lived in a temporary shelter made from snow called an <i><a href="/wiki/Igloo" title="Igloo">igloo</a></i>, and during the few months of the year when temperatures were above freezing, they lived in tents, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Tupiq" title="Tupiq">tupiq</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-inudict_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-inudict-82">[82]</a></sup> made of animal skins supported by a frame of bones or wood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Some, such as the <a href="/wiki/Siglit" title="Siglit">Siglit</a>, used driftwood,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> while others built <a href="/wiki/Sod" title="Sod">sod</a> houses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Gender_roles,_marriage,_birth,_and_community"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Gender_roles.2C_marriage.2C_birth.2C_and_community">Gender roles, marriage, birth, and community</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gender roles, marriage, birth, and community">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Eskimo_kinship" title="Eskimo kinship">Eskimo kinship</a> and <a href="/wiki/Inuit_women" title="Inuit women">Inuit women</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4884" data-file-width="4008" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg/220px-Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg/330px-Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg/440px-Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_Woman_1907_Crisco_edit_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inupiat woman, <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>, circa 1907</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_of_labor" title="Division of labor">division of labor</a> in traditional Inuit society had a strong gender component, but it was not absolute. The men were traditionally hunters and fishermen and the women took care of the children, cleaned the home, sewed, processed food, and cooked. However, there are numerous examples of women who hunted, out of necessity or as a personal choice. At the same time men, who could be away from camp for several days at a time, would be expected to know how to sew and cook.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>The marital customs among the Inuit were not strictly <a href="/wiki/Monogamy" title="Monogamy">monogamous</a>: many Inuit relationships were implicitly or explicitly sexual. <a href="/wiki/Open_marriage" title="Open marriage">Open marriages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polygamy" title="Polygamy">polygamy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Divorce" title="Divorce">divorce</a>, and remarriage were known. Among some Inuit groups, if there were children, divorce required the approval of the community and particularly the agreement of the elders. Marriages were often <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged</a>, sometimes <a href="/wiki/Child_marriage" title="Child marriage">in infancy</a>, and occasionally <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forced on the couple</a> by the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak,_Alaska,_1929,_Edward_S._Curtis_(restored).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2872" data-file-width="3751" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg/220px-Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg/330px-Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg/440px-Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak%2C_Alaska%2C_1929%2C_Edward_S._Curtis_%28restored%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inupiat_Family_from_Noatak,_Alaska,_1929,_Edward_S._Curtis_(restored).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An Inupiat family from <a href="/wiki/Noatak,_Alaska" title="Noatak, Alaska">Noatak, Alaska</a>, 1929.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Marriage was common for women at puberty and for men when they became productive hunters. <a href="/wiki/Family" title="Family">Family</a> structure was flexible: a household might consist of a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_family" title="Nuclear family">man and his wife</a> (or wives) and children; it might include his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Complex_family" title="Complex family">parents or his wife's parents</a> as well as adopted children; it might be a <a href="/wiki/Extended_family" title="Extended family">larger formation</a> of several siblings with their parents, wives and children; or even more than one family sharing dwellings and resources. Every household had its head, an elder or a particularly respected man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>There was also a larger notion of community as, generally, several families shared a place where they wintered. Goods were shared within a household, and also, to a significant extent, within a whole community.</p>
<p>The Inuit were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunter%E2%80%93gatherer" title="Hunter–gatherer">hunter–gatherers</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> and have been referred to as <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> One of the customs following the birth of an infant was for an <i><a href="/wiki/Angakkuq" title="Angakkuq">Angakkuq</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shaman</a>) to place a tiny <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a> carving of a whale into the baby's mouth, in hopes this would make the child good at hunting. Loud singing and drumming were also customary after a birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Raiding">Raiding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Raiding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Virtually all Inuit cultures have <a href="/wiki/Oral_tradition" title="Oral tradition">oral traditions</a> of raids by other indigenous peoples, including fellow Inuit, and of taking vengeance on them in return, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bloody_Falls_Massacre" title="Bloody Falls Massacre">Bloody Falls Massacre</a>. Western observers often regarded these tales as generally not entirely accurate historical accounts, but more as self-serving myths. However, evidence shows that Inuit cultures had quite accurate methods of teaching historical accounts to each new generation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-burch_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-burch-93">[93]</a></sup> In northern Canada, historically there were ethnic feuds between the <a href="/wiki/Dene" title="Dene">Dene</a> and the Inuit, as witnessed by <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hearne" title="Samuel Hearne">Samuel Hearne</a> in 1771.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> In 1996, Dene and Inuit representatives participated in a healing ceremony to reconcile the centuries-old grievances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>The historic accounts of violence against outsiders does make clear that there was a history of hostile contact within the Inuit cultures and with other cultures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> It also makes it clear that Inuit nations existed through history, as well as confederations of such nations. The known confederations were usually formed to defend against a more prosperous, and thus stronger, nation. Alternately, people who lived in less productive geographical areas tended to be less warlike, as they had to spend more time producing food.</p>
<p>Justice within Inuit culture was moderated by the form of governance that gave significant power to the elders. As in most cultures around the world, justice could be harsh and often included capital punishment for serious crimes against the community or the individual. During raids against other peoples, the Inuit, like their non-Inuit neighbors, tended to be merciless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-attituq_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-attituq-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Suicide,_murder,_and_death"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Suicide.2C_murder.2C_and_death">Suicide, murder, and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Suicide, murder, and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><span id="Suicide"></span></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Suicide_in_Greenland" title="Suicide in Greenland">Suicide in Greenland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suicide_in_Canada#Among_aboriginal_people" title="Suicide in Canada">Suicide among Canadian aboriginal people</a></div>
<p>A pervasive European myth about Inuit is that they killed elderly (<a href="/wiki/Senicide" title="Senicide">senicide</a>) and "unproductive people",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> but this is not generally true.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In a culture with an <a href="/wiki/Oral_history" title="Oral history">oral history</a>, elders are the keepers of communal knowledge, effectively the community library.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nso0_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-nso0-102">[102]</a></sup> Because they are of extreme value as the repository of knowledge, there are cultural <a href="/wiki/Taboo" title="Taboo">taboos</a> against sacrificing elders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In Antoon A. Leenaar's book <i>Suicide in Canada</i> he states that "<a href="/wiki/Knud_Rasmussen" title="Knud Rasmussen">Rasmussen</a> found that the death of elders by suicide was a commonplace among the Iglulik Inuit."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leenaars196_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-leenaars196-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Franz_Boas" title="Franz Boas">Franz Boas</a>, suicide was "...not of rare occurrence..." and was generally accomplished through hanging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Writing of the Labrador Inuit, Hawkes (1916) was considerably more explicit on the subject of suicide and the burden of the elderly:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Aged people who have outlived their usefulness and whose life is a burden both to themselves and their relatives are put to death by stabbing or strangulation. This is customarily done at the request of the individual concerned, but not always so. Aged people who are a hindrance on the trail are abandoned.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Antoon A. Leenaars, <i>Suicide in Canada</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-leenaars195_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-leenaars195-107">[107]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>When food is not sufficient, the elderly are the least likely to survive. In the extreme case of <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, the Inuit fully understood that, if there was to be any hope of obtaining more food, a hunter was necessarily the one to feed on whatever food was left. However, a common response to desperate conditions and the threat of starvation was <a href="/wiki/Infanticide" title="Infanticide">infanticide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> A mother abandoned an infant in hopes that someone less desperate might find and adopt the child before the cold or animals killed it.
