The belief that the Inuit regularly resorted to infanticide may be due in part to studies done by Asen Balikci,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> Milton Freeman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> and David Riches<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> among the Netsilik, along with the trial of <a href="/wiki/Kikkik" title="Kikkik">Kikkik</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Other recent research has noted that "While there is little disagreement that there were examples of infanticide in Inuit communities, it is presently not known the depth and breadth of these incidents. The research is neither complete nor conclusive to allow for a determination of whether infanticide was a rare or a widely practiced event."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anthropologists believed that Inuit cultures routinely killed children born with physical defects because of the demands of the extreme climate. These views were changed by late 20th century discoveries of burials at an archaeological site. Between 1982 and 1994, a storm with high winds caused ocean waves to erode part of the bluffs near <a href="/wiki/Barrow,_Alaska" title="Barrow, Alaska">Barrow, Alaska</a>, and a body was discovered to have been washed out of the mud. Unfortunately the storm claimed the body, which was not recovered. But examination of the eroded bank indicated that an ancient house, perhaps with other remains, was likely to be claimed by the next storm. The site, known as the "Ukkuqsi archaeological site", was excavated. Several frozen bodies (now known as the "frozen family") were recovered, autopsies were performed, and they were re-interred as the first burials in the then-new Imaiqsaun Cemetery south of Barrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> Years later another body was washed out of the bluff. It was a female child, approximately 9 years old, who had clearly been born with a <a href="/wiki/Congenital_disorder" title="Congenital disorder">congenital birth defect</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> This child had never been able to walk, but must have been cared for by family throughout her life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deargirl_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-deargirl-118">[118]</a></sup> She was the best preserved body ever recovered in Alaska, and radiocarbon dating of grave goods and of a strand of her hair all place her back to about 1200 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deargirl_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-deargirl-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the 19th century, the Western Arctic suffered a population decline of close to 90%, resulting from exposure to new diseases, including <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>. Autopsies near Greenland reveal that, more commonly <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nephrology" title="Nephrology">kidney diseases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Trichinosis" title="Trichinosis">trichinosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malnutrition" title="Malnutrition">malnutrition</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Degenerative_disease" title="Degenerative disease">degenerative disorders</a> may have contributed to mass deaths among different Inuit tribes. The Inuit believed that the causes of the disease were of a spiritual origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_law">Traditional law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Traditional law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inuit traditional laws are anthropologically different from <a href="/wiki/Western_law" title="Western law">Western law</a> concepts. <a href="/wiki/Custom_(law)" title="Custom (law)">Customary law</a> was thought non-existent in Inuit society before the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_Canada" title="Law of Canada">Canadian legal system</a>. <a href="/wiki/E._Adamson_Hoebel" title="E. Adamson Hoebel">Hoebel</a>, in 1954, concluded that only 'rudimentary law' existed amongst the Inuit. Indeed, prior to about 1970, it is impossible to find even one reference to a Western observer who was aware that any form of governance existed among any Inuit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tpm_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-tpm-120">[120]</a></sup> however, there was a set way of doing things that had to be followed:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>maligait</i> refers to what has to be followed</li>
<li><i>piqujait</i> refers to what has to be done</li>
<li><i>tirigusuusiit</i> refers to what has to be avoided</li>
</ul>
<p>If an individual's actions went against the tirigusuusiit, maligait or piqujait, the <a href="/wiki/Angakkuq" title="Angakkuq">angakkuq</a> (shaman) might have to intervene, lest the consequences be dire to the individual or the community.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We are told today that Inuit never had laws or "maligait". Why? They say because they are not written on paper. When I think of paper, I think you can tear it up, and the laws are gone. The laws of the Inuit are not on paper.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Mariano Aupilaarjuk, Rankin Inlet, Nunavut, <i>Perspectives on Traditional Law</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Traditional_beliefs">Traditional beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Traditional beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_mythology" title="Inuit mythology">Inuit mythology</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shamanism_among_Eskimo_peoples" title="Shamanism among Eskimo peoples">Shamanism among Eskimo peoples</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Polarlicht.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1960" data-file-width="3008" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Polarlicht.jpg/220px-Polarlicht.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Polarlicht.jpg/330px-Polarlicht.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Polarlicht.jpg/440px-Polarlicht.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Polarlicht.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Some Inuit believed that the spirits of their ancestors could be seen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora borealis</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The environment in which the Inuit lived inspired a <a href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a> filled with adventure tales of whale and walrus hunts. Long winter months of waiting for caribou herds or sitting near breathing holes hunting seals gave birth to stories of mysterious and sudden appearance of ghosts and fantastic creatures. Some Inuit looked into the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurora borealis</a></i>, or northern lights, to find images of their family and friends dancing in the next life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> However, some Inuit believed that the lights were more sinister and if you <a href="/wiki/Whistling" title="Whistling">whistled</a> at them, they would come down and cut off your head. This tale is still told to children today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAAE_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-CAAE-124">[124]</a></sup> For others they were invisible giants, the souls of animals, a guide to hunting and as a spirit for the <i>angakkuq</i> to help with healing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CAAE_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-CAAE-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> They relied upon the <i>angakkuq</i> (shaman) for spiritual interpretation. The nearest thing to a central deity was the Old Woman (<i><a href="/wiki/Sedna_(mythology)" title="Sedna (mythology)">Sedna</a></i>), who lived beneath the sea. The waters, a central food source, were believed to contain great gods.</p>
<p>The Inuit practiced a form of shamanism based on <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animist</a> principles. They believed that all things had a form of spirit, including humans, and that to some extent these spirits could be influenced by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> of supernatural entities that could be appeased when one required some animal or inanimate thing to act in a certain way. The <i>angakkuq</i> of a community of Inuit was not the leader, but rather a sort of healer and <a href="/wiki/Psychotherapy" title="Psychotherapy">psychotherapist</a>, who tended wounds and offered advice, as well as invoking the spirits to assist people in their lives. His or her role was to see, interpret and exhort the subtle and unseen. <i>Angakkuit</i> were not trained; they were held to be born with the ability and recognized by the community as they approached adulthood.</p>
<p>Inuit religion was closely tied to a system of rituals integrated into the daily life of the people. These rituals were simple but held to be necessary. According to a customary Inuit saying,</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The great peril of our existence lies in the fact that our diet consists entirely of souls.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>By believing that all things, including animals, have souls like those of humans, any hunt that failed to show appropriate respect and customary supplication would only give the liberated spirits cause to avenge themselves.</p>
<p>The harshness and unpredictability of life in the Arctic ensured that Inuit lived with concern for the uncontrollable, where a streak of bad luck could destroy an entire community. To offend a spirit was to risk its interference with an already marginal existence. The Inuit understood that they had to work in harmony with supernatural powers to provide the necessities of day-to-day life. Before the 1940s, Inuit had minimal contact with Europeans, who passed through on their way to hunt whales or <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">trade furs</a> but seldom had any interest in settling down on the frozen land of the Arctic. So the Inuit had the place to themselves. They moved between summer and winter camps to always be living where there were animals to hunt.</p>
<p>But that changed. As <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> ended and the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> began, the Arctic became a place where countries that did not get along were close to each other. The Arctic had always been seen as inaccessible, but the invention of aircraft made it easier for non-Arctic dwellers to get there. As new <a href="/wiki/Distant_Early_Warning_Line" title="Distant Early Warning Line">airbases and radar stations</a> were built in the Arctic to monitor rival nations, permanent settlements were developed around them, including schools and health care centres. In many places, Inuit children were required to attend schools that emphasized non-native traditions. With better health care, the Inuit population grew too large to sustain itself solely by hunting.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2010)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many Inuit from smaller camps moved into permanent settlements because there was access to jobs and food. In many areas<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Naming_conventions_(geographic_names)" title="Wikipedia:Naming conventions (geographic names)"><span title="The geographic scope near this tag is ambiguous. (July 2011)">where?</span></a></i>]</sup> Inuit were required to live in towns by the 1960s.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In total there are about 134,241 Inuit living in four countries, Canada, Greenland, Denmark, and the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caninu_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-caninu-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geinu_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-geinu-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dkinu_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-dkinu-4">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usinu_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-usinu-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada_2">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the 50,480<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-caninu_2-3"><a href="#cite_note-caninu-2">[2]</a></sup> Inuit listed in the <a href="/wiki/Canada_2006_Census" title="Canada 2006 Census">2006 Canada Census</a> can be found throughout Canada the majority, 44,470, live in four regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nlinu_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-nlinu-126">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntinu_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-ntinu-127">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuinu_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-nuinu-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qcinu_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-qcinu-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of the 2006 Canada Census there were 4,715 Inuit living in <a href="/wiki/Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Newfoundland and Labrador">Newfoundland and Labrador</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nlinu_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-nlinu-126">[126]</a></sup> and about 2,160 live in <a href="/wiki/Nunatsiavut" title="Nunatsiavut">Nunatsiavut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> There are also about 6,000 <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut_people" title="NunatuKavut people">NunatuKavut people</a> (Labrador Metis or Inuit-metis) living in southern <a href="/wiki/Labrador" title="Labrador">Labrador</a> in what is called <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of the 2006 Canada Census there were 4,165 Inuit living in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Territories" title="Northwest Territories">Northwest Territories</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ntinu_127-1"><a href="#cite_note-ntinu-127">[127]</a></sup> The majority, about 3,115, live in the six communities of the <a href="/wiki/Inuvialuit_Settlement_Region" title="Inuvialuit Settlement Region">Inuvialuit Settlement Region</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of the 2006 Canada Census there were 24,640 Inuit living in <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nuinu_128-1"><a href="#cite_note-nuinu-128">[128]</a></sup> In Nunavut the Inuit population forms a majority in all communities and is the only <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">jurisdiction of Canada</a> where Aboriginal peoples form a majority.</p>
<p>As of the 2006 Canada Census there were 10,950 Inuit living in <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-qcinu_129-1"><a href="#cite_note-qcinu-129">[129]</a></sup> The majority, about 9,565, live in <a href="/wiki/Nunavik" title="Nunavik">Nunavik</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland">Greenland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Greenland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_Inuit" title="Greenlandic Inuit">Greenlandic Inuit</a></div>
<p>According to the 2013 edition of <i><a href="/wiki/The_World_Factbook" title="The World Factbook">The World Factbook</a></i>, published by the <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a>, the Inuit population of Greenland is 89% (51,365) out of a total of 57,714 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geinu_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-geinu-1">[1]</a></sup> Like Nunavut the population lives throughout the region.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Denmark">Denmark</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Denmark">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The population size of <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_people_in_Denmark" title="Greenlandic people in Denmark">Greenlandic people in Denmark</a> varies from source to source between 15,000 and 20,000. According to 2015 figures from <a href="/wiki/Statistics_Denmark" title="Statistics Denmark">Statistics Denmark</a> there are 15,815 people residing in Denmark of Greenlandic Inuit ancestry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dkinu_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-dkinu-4">[4]</a></sup> Most travel to Denmark for educational purposes, and many remain after finishing their education ,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gebra_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-gebra-134">[134]</a></sup> which results in the population being mostly concentrated in the big 4 educational cities of <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen" title="Copenhagen">Copenhagen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aarhus" title="Aarhus">Aarhus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Odense" title="Odense">Odense</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aalborg" title="Aalborg">Aalborg</a>, which all have vibrant Greenlandic communities and cultural centers (Kalaallit Illuutaat).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/2000_United_States_Census" title="2000 United States Census">2000 United States Census</a> there were a total of 16,581 Inuit/Inupiat living throughout the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usinu_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-usinu-3">[3]</a></sup> The majority, about 14,718, live in the state of <a href="/wiki/Alaska" title="Alaska">Alaska</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-akinu_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-akinu-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russia">Russia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Russia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the 2010 Russian Census there were a total of 1,738 Inuit/Eskimo living throughout the country, mostly in the East of the <a href="/wiki/Far_Eastern_Federal_District" title="Far Eastern Federal District">Far Eastern Federal District</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Governance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_conf_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Inuit_conf_map.png/300px-Inuit_conf_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Inuit_conf_map.png/450px-Inuit_conf_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3a/Inuit_conf_map.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inuit_conf_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map showing the members of the Inuit Circumpolar Conference.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Circumpolar_Council" title="Inuit Circumpolar Council">Inuit Circumpolar Council</a> is a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>-recognized <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organization</a> (NGO), which defines its constituency as Canada's Inuit and <a href="/wiki/Inuvialuit" title="Inuvialuit">Inuvialuit</a>, Greenland's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kalaallit_Inuit" title="Kalaallit Inuit">Kalaallit Inuit</a>, Alaska's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inupiat" title="Inupiat">Inupiat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yup%27ik" title="Yup'ik">Yup'ik</a>, and Russia's <a href="/wiki/Siberian_Yupik" title="Siberian Yupik">Siberian Yupik</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICCcharter_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICCcharter-137">[137]</a></sup> despite the last two neither speaking an Inuit dialect<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kaplan_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-kaplan-11">[11]</a></sup> or considering themselves "Inuit". Nonetheless, it has come together with other circumpolar cultural and political groups to promote the Inuit and other northern people in their fight against <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> problems such as <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> which disproportionately affects the Inuit population. The <a href="/wiki/Inuit_Circumpolar_Council" title="Inuit Circumpolar Council">Inuit Circumpolar Council</a> is one of the six group of Arctic indigenous peoples that have a seat as a so-called "Permanent Participant" on the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Council" title="Arctic Council">Arctic Council</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> an international high level forum in which the eight Arctic Countries (USA, Canada, Russia, Denmark, Iceland, Norway, Sweden and Finland) discuss Arctic policy. On 12 May 2011, Greenland's Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Kuupik_Kleist" title="Kuupik Kleist">Kuupik Kleist</a> hosted the ministerial meeting of the Arctic Council, an event for which the American Secretary of State <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a> came to Nuuk, as did many other high-ranking officials such as Russian Foreign Minister <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sergei_Lavrov" title="Sergei Lavrov">Sergei Lavrov</a>, Swedish Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Carl_Bildt" title="Carl Bildt">Carl Bildt</a> and Norwegian Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Jonas_Gahr_St%C3%B8re" title="Jonas Gahr Støre">Jonas Gahr Støre</a>. At that event they signed the Nuuk Declaration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_autonomy_in_Canada">Regional autonomy in Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Regional autonomy in Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Nunavut" title="Nunavut">Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunavik" title="Nunavik">Nunavik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nunatsiavut" title="Nunatsiavut">Nunatsiavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Inuvik_Region" title="Inuvik Region">Inuvik Region</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map-regions-inuit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="205" data-file-width="265" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Map-regions-inuit.jpg/220px-Map-regions-inuit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Map-regions-inuit.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map-regions-inuit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of all Inuit regions</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Inuvialuit" title="Inuvialuit">Inuvialuit</a> are western Canadian Inuit who remained in the Northwest Territories when Nunavut split off. They live primarily in the Mackenzie River delta, on <a href="/wiki/Banks_Island" title="Banks Island">Banks Island</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Island_(Canada)" title="Victoria Island (Canada)">Victoria Island</a> in the Northwest Territories. They are officially represented by the Inuvialuit Regional Corporation and, in 1984, received a comprehensive land claims settlement, the first in Northern Canada, with the signing of the Inuvialuit Final Agreement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>The TFN worked for ten years and, in September 1992, came to a final agreement with the Government of Canada. This agreement called for the separation of the Northwest Territories into an eastern territory whose Aboriginal population would be predominately Inuit,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> the future Nunavut, and a rump Northwest Territories in the west. It was the largest <a href="/wiki/Land_claim" title="Land claim">land claims</a> agreement in Canadian history. In November 1992, the <i>Nunavut Final Agreement</i> was approved by nearly 85% of the Inuit of what would become Nunavut. As the final step in this long process, the <i><a href="/wiki/Nunavut_Land_Claims_Agreement" title="Nunavut Land Claims Agreement">Nunavut Land Claims Agreement</a></i> was signed on May 25, 1993, in Iqaluit by Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Brian_Mulroney" title="Brian Mulroney">Brian Mulroney</a> and by Paul Quassa, the president of <a href="/wiki/Nunavut_Tunngavik_Incorporated" title="Nunavut Tunngavik Incorporated">Nunavut Tunngavik Incorporated</a>, which replaced the TFN with the ratification of the Nunavut Final Agreement. The <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Canada" title="Parliament of Canada">Canadian Parliament</a> passed the supporting legislation in June of the same year, enabling the 1999 establishment of Nunavut as a <a href="/wiki/Territorial_entity" title="Territorial entity">territorial entity</a>.</p>
<p>With the establishment of Nunatsiavut in 2005, almost all the traditional Inuit lands in Canada, with the exception <a href="/wiki/NunatuKavut" title="NunatuKavut">NunatuKavut</a> in central and South Labrador, are now covered by some sort of land claims agreement providing for regional autonomy.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Greenland_2">Greenland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Greenland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="300" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg/220px-Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg/330px-Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg/440px-Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenland-municipalities-2009.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Municipalities_of_Greenland" title="Municipalities of Greenland">Municipalities of Greenland</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kalaallit" title="Kalaallit">Kalaallit</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Greenland" title="History of Greenland">History of Greenland</a></div>
<p>In 1953, Denmark put an end to the colonial status of Greenland and granted <a href="/wiki/Home_rule" title="Home rule">home rule</a> in 1979 and in 2008 a <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_self-government_referendum,_2008" title="Greenlandic self-government referendum, 2008">self-government referendum</a> was passed with 75% approval. Although still a part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danish_Realm" title="Danish Realm">Kingdom of Denmark</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Denmark" title="Denmark">Denmark</a> proper and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>), Greenland, known as Kalaallit Nunaat in the <a href="/wiki/Greenlandic_language" title="Greenlandic language">Greenlandic language</a>, maintains much autonomy today. Of a population of 56,000, 80% of Greenlanders identify as Inuit. Their economy is based on <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shrimp_fishery" title="Shrimp fishery">shrimping</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Thule people arrived in Greenland in the 13th century. There they encountered the Norsemen, who had established colonies there since the late 10th century, as well as a later wave of the Dorset people. Because most of Greenland is covered in ice, the Greenland Inuit (or Kalaallit) only live in coastal settlements, particularly the northern polar coast, the eastern Amassalik coast and the central coasts of western Greenland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alaska">Alaska</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Alaska">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="816" data-file-width="1100" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg/300px-ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg/450px-ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg/600px-ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ANCSA_Regional_Corporations_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alaska Native Regional Corporations</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_Regional_Corporations" title="Alaska Native Regional Corporations">Alaska Native Regional Corporations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russian_America" title="Russian America">Russian America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Statehood_Act" title="Alaska Statehood Act">Alaska Statehood Act</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Alaska_Native_tribal_entities" title="List of Alaska Native tribal entities">List of Alaska Native tribal entities</a></div>
<p>Currently Alaska is governed as a State within United States with very <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_Regional_Corporations" title="Alaska Native Regional Corporations">limited autonomy</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native" title="Alaska Native">Alaska Native</a> peoples. European Colonization of Alaska started in the 18th century by Russia. By the 1860s, the Russian government was considering ridding itself of its <a href="/wiki/Russian_America" title="Russian America">Russian America</a> colony. Alaska was officially <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Statehood_Act" title="Alaska Statehood Act">incorporated</a> to United States on January 3, 1959.</p>
<p>The Inuit of Alaska are the Inupiat (from Inuit- people – and piaq/piat real, i.e. 'real people') who live in the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Arctic_Borough,_Alaska" title="Northwest Arctic Borough, Alaska">Northwest Arctic Borough</a>, the <a href="/wiki/North_Slope_Borough,_Alaska" title="North Slope Borough, Alaska">North Slope Borough</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">Bering Straits</a> region. Barrow, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_points_of_the_United_States" title="Extreme points of the United States">northernmost city in the United States</a>, is in the Inupiat region. Their language is <a href="/wiki/Inupiaq_language" title="Inupiaq language">Iñupiaq</a> (which is the singular form of Inupiat).</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_culture">Modern culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Inuit&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Modern culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Femmes_Nain.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="474" data-file-width="641" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Femmes_Nain.jpg/220px-Femmes_Nain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Femmes_Nain.jpg/330px-Femmes_Nain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Femmes_Nain.jpg/440px-Femmes_Nain.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Femmes_Nain.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inuit women at <a href="/wiki/Nain,_Newfoundland_and_Labrador" title="Nain, Newfoundland and Labrador">Nain, Newfoundland and Labrador</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Inuit_art" title="Inuit art">Inuit art</a>, carving, print making, textiles and <a href="/wiki/Inuit_throat_singing" title="Inuit throat singing">Inuit throat singing</a>, are very popular, not only in Canada but globally, and Inuit artists are widely known. Canada has adopted some of the Inuit culture as national symbols, using Inuit cultural icons like the inukshuk in unlikely places, such as its use as a symbol at the <a href="/wiki/2010_Winter_Olympics" title="2010 Winter Olympics">2010 Winter Olympics</a> in Vancouver. Respected art galleries display Inuit art, the largest collection of which is at the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Art_Gallery" title="Winnipeg Art Gallery">Winnipeg Art Gallery</a>.</p>
<p>Some Inuit languages such as Inuktitut, appears to have a more secure future in Quebec and Nunavut. There are a surprising number of Inuit, even those who now live in urban centres such as <a href="/wiki/Ottawa" title="Ottawa">Ottawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Montreal" title="Montreal">Montreal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg" title="Winnipeg">Winnipeg</a>, who have experienced living on the land in the traditional life style. People such as <a href="/wiki/Legislative_Assembly_of_Nunavut" title="Legislative Assembly of Nunavut">Legislative Assembly of Nunavut</a> member, <a href="/wiki/Levinia_Brown" title="Levinia Brown">Levinia Brown</a> and former <a href="/wiki/Commissioners_of_Nunavut" title="Commissioners of Nunavut">Commissioner of Nunavut</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Commissioners_of_Northwest_Territories" title="Commissioners of Northwest Territories">NWT</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helen_Maksagak" title="Helen Maksagak">Helen Maksagak</a> were born and lived the early part of their life "on the land". Inuit culture is alive and vibrant today in spite of the negative impacts of recent history.</p>
<p>An important biennial event, the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Winter_Games" title="Arctic Winter Games">Arctic Winter Games</a>, is held in communities across the northern regions of the world, featuring traditional Inuit and northern sports as part of the events. A cultural event is also held. The games were first held in 1970, and while rotated usually among Alaska, Yukon and the Northwest Territories, they have also been held in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schefferville,_Quebec" title="Schefferville, Quebec">Schefferville, Quebec</a> in 1976, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_Lake,_Alberta" title="Slave Lake, Alberta">Slave Lake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alberta" title="Alberta">Alberta</a>, and a joint Iqaluit, Nunavut-<a href="/wiki/Nuuk" title="Nuuk">Nuuk</a>, Greenland staging in 2002. In other sporting events, <a href="/wiki/Jordin_Tootoo" title="Jordin Tootoo">Jordin Tootoo</a> became the first Inuk to play in the <a href="/wiki/National_Hockey_League" title="National Hockey League">National Hockey League</a> in the 2003–04 season, playing for the <a href="/wiki/Nashville_Predators" title="Nashville Predators">Nashville Predators</a>.</p>
<p>Although Inuit life has changed significantly over the past century, many traditions continue. <i><a href="/wiki/Inuit_Qaujimajatuqangit" title="Inuit Qaujimajatuqangit">Inuit Qaujimajatuqangit</a></i>, or traditional knowledge, such as storytelling, mythology, <a href="/wiki/Inuit_music" title="Inuit music">music</a> and dancing remain important parts of the culture. Family and community are very important. The Inuktitut language is still spoken in many areas of the Arctic and is common on radio and in television programming.</p>
<p>Well-known Inuit politicians include <a href="/wiki/Premier_of_Nunavut" title="Premier of Nunavut">Premier of Nunavut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Taptuna" title="Peter Taptuna">Peter Taptuna</a>, Nancy Karetak-Lindell, former MP for the <a href="/wiki/Nunavut_(electoral_district)" title="Nunavut (electoral district)">riding of Nunavut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kuupik_Kleist" title="Kuupik Kleist">Kuupik Kleist</a>, Prime Minister of Greenland. Leona Aglukkaq, current MP, was the first Inuk to be sworn into the Canadian Federal Cabinet as Health Minister in 2008. In May 2011 after being re-elected for her second term, Ms. Aglukkaq was given the additional portfolio of Minister of the Canadian Northern Economic Development Agency. In July 2013 she was sworn in as the Minister of the Environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="592" data-file-width="1024" height="127" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg/220px-Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg/330px-Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg/440px-Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greenland_kayak_seal_hunter_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Inuit seal hunter in a kayak, armed with a harpoon.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Visual and performing arts are strong. In 2002 the first <a href="/wiki/Feature_film" title="Feature film">feature film</a> in Inuktitut, <i><a href="/wiki/Atanarjuat:_The_Fast_Runner" title="Atanarjuat: The Fast Runner">Atanarjuat: The Fast Runner</a></i>, was released worldwide to great critical and popular acclaim. It was directed by <a href="/wiki/Zacharias_Kunuk" title="Zacharias Kunuk">Zacharias Kunuk</a>, and written, filmed, produced, directed, and acted almost entirely by the Inuit of Igloolik. In 2009, the film <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Le_Voyage_D%27Inuk" title="Le Voyage D'Inuk">Le Voyage D'Inuk</a>, a Greenlandic language feature film, was <a href="/wiki/Film_director" title="Film director">directed</a> by Mike Magidson and co-written by Magidson and French <a href="/wiki/Film_producer" title="Film producer">film producer</a> Jean-Michel Huctin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-R0_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-R0-145">[145]</a></sup> One of the most famous Inuit artists is <a href="/wiki/Pitseolak_Ashoona" title="Pitseolak Ashoona">Pitseolak Ashoona</a>. <a href="/wiki/Susan_Aglukark" title="Susan Aglukark">Susan Aglukark</a> is a popular singer. <a href="/wiki/Mitiarjuk_Nappaaluk" title="Mitiarjuk Nappaaluk">Mitiarjuk Attasie Nappaaluk</a> worked at preserving Inuktitut and wrote one of the first novels ever published in that language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> In 2006, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Dorset,_Nunavut" title="Cape Dorset, Nunavut">Cape Dorset</a> was hailed as Canada's most artistic city, with 23% of the labor force employed in the arts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Inuit art such as soapstone carvings is one of Nunavut's most important industries.</p>
<p>Recently, there has been an identity struggle among the younger generations of Inuit, between their traditional heritage and the modern society which their cultures have been forced to assimilate into in order to maintain a livelihood. With current dependence on modern society for necessities, (including governmental jobs, food, aid, medicine, etc.), the Inuit have had much interaction with and exposure to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norm_(sociology)" title="Norm (sociology)">societal norms</a> outside their previous cultural boundaries. The stressors regarding the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Identity_crisis_(psychology)" title="Identity crisis (psychology)">identity crisis</a> among teenagers have led to disturbingly high numbers of suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-analysis_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-analysis-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>A series of authors has focused upon the increasing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Myopia" title="Myopia">myopia</a> in the youngest generations of Inuit. Myopia was almost unknown prior to the Inuit adoption of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">western culture</a>. Principal theories are the change to a western style diet with more refined foods, and extended education.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Pisurayak_Kootook" title="David Pisurayak Kootook">David Pisurayak Kootook</a> was awarded the Meritorious Service Cross, posthumously, for his heroic efforts in a 1972 plane crash.</p>
